
# Regularly Sold

### 221 reprints from 1856-08-21 to 1892-08-21

## [Regularly Sold](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn83045462/1856-08-21/ed-1/seq-1/)

#### From _Evening Star_

#### 1856-08-21

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

REGULARLY SOLD—During the month of January, 1850, two gentlemen from New York, one of whom had been in California nearly a year, and the other just arrived, were accidentally overheard in the following conversation at the Sutter House, Sacramento. The new comer was lamenting his condition, and his folly in leaving an abundance at home, and especially two beautiful daughters, who were just budding into womanhood—when he asked the other if he had a family.  
  
&quot;Yes, sir; I have a wife and six children in New York—and I never saw one of them.&quot;  
  
After this reply the couple sat a few moments in silence, and then the interrogator again commenced:  
  
&quot;Were you ever blind, sir?&quot;  
  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;    
  
&quot;Did you marry a widow, sir?&quot;  
  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;  
  
Another lapse of silence.  
  
&quot;Did I understand you to say, sir, that you had a wife and six children living in New York, and had never seen one of them?&quot;  
  
&quot;Yes sir—I so stated it.&quot;  
  
Another and longer pause of silence. Then the interrogator again inquired:  
  
&quot;How can it be, sir, that you never saw one of them?&quot;  
  
&quot;Why,&quot; was the response, &quot;*one of them* was born after I left.&quot;  
  
&quot;Oh! ah!&quot; and a general laugh followed.   
  
After that the first New Yorker was especially distinguished as &quot;the man who has six children and never saw one of them.&quot;  

</td></tr></table>

---

## [From _New-York dispatch._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn83030364/1856-08-31/ed-1/seq-7)

#### 1856-08-31 &middot; [New York [N.Y.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
During the month of January, 1850, two gentlemen from  
New York, one of whom had been in California nearly a  
year, and the other just arrived, were accidentally over­  
heard in the following conversation at the Sutter House,  
Sacremento. The new comer was lamenting his condition,  
and his folly for leaving an abundance at home, and espe­  
cially two beautiful daughters, who were just budding into  
womanhood, when he asked the other if he had a family.  
“Yes, sir, I have. I have a wife and six children in New  
York, and I never saw one of them !” After this reply the  
couple sat a few moments in silence, and then the interro­  
gator again commenced: “Were you ever blind, sir?”  
“No, sir.” Another lapse of silence. “ Did you marry a  
widow, sir?” “No, sir.” Another lapse of silence.  
“Did I understand you to say, sir, that you had a wife and  
six children living in New York, and had never seen one  
of them?” “Yes, sir; I so stated it.” Another and a  
longer pause of silence. Then the interrogator again in­  
quired : “How can it be, sir, that you never saw one of  
them?” “Why,” was the response, “ one of them was  
born after I left.” “Oh lab!” and a general laugh fol­  
lowed. After that the first New Yorker was especially dis­  
tinguished as “ the man who has six children, and never  
saw one of them.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/iiif/2/dlc_collins_ver01%2Fdata%2Fsn83030364%2Fprint00211110065%2F1856083101%2F0007.jp2/pct:69.175222,15.174904,12.001914,7.347815/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

## From _Houma Ceres._

#### 1856-09-27 &middot; [Houma, Parish of Terrebonne, La.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Houma%2C_Louisiana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
ReoULArLY SoLd.-During the month  
of January, 1850, two gentlemen from  
New York, one of whom had been in  
California nearly a year, and the other  
just arrived, were accidentally overheard  
in the following conversation at the Sutter  
House, Sacramento. The new comer  
was lamenting his condition, and his folly  
in leaving an abundance at home, and es­  
pecially two beautiful daughters who were  
just budding into womanhood--when he  
asked the other if he had a family?  
&quot;Yes, sir, I have. I have a wife and  
six children in New York-and I never  
saw one of them!&quot;  
After this reply the couple sat a few  
moments in silence, and then the interro­  
gator again commenced:  
&quot;Were you ever blind, sir?&quot;  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;  
&quot;Did you marry a widow, sir?&quot;  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;  
Another lapse of silence.  
&quot;Did I understand you to say, sir, that  
you had a wife and six children living in  
New York, and had never seen one o  
them?&quot;  
&quot;Yes, sir--I so stated it.&quot;  
Another and a longer pause of silence.  
Then the interrogator again inquired:  
&quot;How can it be, sir, that you never saw  
one of them?&quot;  
&quot;Why,&quot; was the respouse, &quot;one of  
them was born after I left.&quot;  
&quot;Oh! ah!&quot; and a general laugh fol.  
lowed.  
After that the first New Yorker was  
especially distinguished as &quot;the man who  
has six children and never saw one o
</td></tr></table>

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
ramento city California, I accidentally over­  
heard a conversation between two gentlemen,  
one of whom was from New York city, and  
had been in the country nearly a year, and  
the other had just arrived.  
The newcomer was lamenting his condi­  
tion and his folly for leaving an abundance  
at home, and especially two beautiful daugh­  
ters, who were just blooming into woman­  
hood—when he asked the New Yorker if he  
had a family.  
“Yes, sir ; I have a wife and six ehild­  
rrn in N. York—and I never saw one of  
them.”  
After this reply, the couple sat a few mo­  
ments in silence; then the interrogator again ’  
commenced:  
“Was you ever blind, sir Y”  
“No, sir.”  
“Did you marry a widow, sir Y”  
“No, sir.”  
Another lapse of silence.  
“Did I understood you to say, sir, that  
you had a wife and six children living in  
New York, and had never seen one of them.”  
“Yes, sir—I so stated it.”  
Another and a longer pause of silence.—  
Then the interrogator again inquired :  
“How can it be, sir, that you never seen  
one ofthem ?”  
“Why,” was the response, “e?ic of them  
was born after I left.”  
“Oh ! ah !” and a general laugh followed;  
and after that the New Yorker was especi­  
ally distinguished as the man who had “had  
six children and never saw one of them.”

## [From _San Andreas independent._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn85025090/1856-11-08/ed-1/seq-4)

.  
“Insult on insult!” shouted the infuri­  
ated man, and he rushed at the poor fel­  
low with the evident intention of ejecting  
him, but was restrained by the voice of his  
daughter, exclaiming—  
“Pappa, I suppose the young man in­  
tended to say that he could not be heard  
on account of the roaring of the dam.”  
“Oh—l beg your pardon, sir—l beg  
your pardon—walk in, walk in, real!}*—  
ah well! I declare! The dam roaring !  
Capital! Come in, come in. That is  
really too rich !”  
It is needless to add that the youngster  
went in, and in the excellent society of the  
young lady, soon forgot the “dam roaring.”  
Regularly Sold.—During the month  
January, 1850, two gentlemen from New  
York, one of whom had been in Califor­  
nia nearly a year, and the other just ar­  
rived, were accidentally overheard in the  
following conversation at the Sutter House,  
Sacramento. The new comer was lamen  
ting his condition, and his folly in leaving  
an abundance at home, and especially two  
beautiful daughters who were just budding  
into womanhood—when he asked the other  
if he had a family.  
“Yes, sir, I have. I have a wife and  
six children in New York—and I never  
saw one of them ! ”  
After this reply the couple sat n few  
moments in silence, and then the interroga­  
tor again commenced:  
“ Were you ever blind, sir?”  
“ No sir.”  
“ Did you marry a widow, sir?”  
“ No sir.”  
Another lapse of silence.  
“ Did I understand you to say, sir, that  
you had a wife and six children in New  
York and had never seen one of them?”  
“ Yes, sir—l so stated it.”  
Another and a longer pause of silence.  
Then the interrogator again inquired :  
“ How can it be, sir, that you never;  
saw one of them?”  
“Why,” was the response, “one of\
them was born after I left.”  
“Oh ! ah I” and a general laugh fol­  
lowed.  
After that the first New Yorker was es­  
pecially distinguished as “ the man who  
had six children and never saw one of1  
them.”

## From _Richmond daily Whig._

#### 1856-11-10 &middot; [Richmond, Va.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Richmond%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
Reoularly Sold--During the month of January, 1850,  
two gentlemen from New York, one of whom had been  
in California nearly a year, and the other just arrived,  
were accidentally overheard in the following conversation  
at the Sutter House, Sacramento. The new comer wa  
lamenting his condition, and his folly in leaving an abun­  
dance at home, and especially two beautiful daughters,  
who were just budding into womanhood,--when he asked  
  
the other it he had a family.  
&quot;Yes, sir; I have a wife and six children in New York  
- and I never saw one of them.&quot;  
After this reply the couples sat a few moments in si­  
lence, and then the interrogator again commenced:  
&quot;Were you ever blind, sir?&quot;  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;  
&quot;Did you marry a widow, sir?&quot;  
&quot;No, sir?&quot;  
Another lapse of silence.  
&quot;Did I understand you to say, sir, that you have a wife  
and six children living in New York, and had never seen  
one of them?&quot;  
&quot;Yes, sir--I so stated it.  
Another and a longer pause of silence. Then the in­  
terrogator again inqu&#x27;red:  
&quot;How can it be, sir, that you never saw one of them.&quot;  
&quot;Why,&quot; was the response, &quot;one of them was born after  
I left.&quot;  
&quot;Oh! ah!&quot; and a general laugh followed.  
After that the first New Yorker was especially distin­  
guished as &quot;the man who has children, and never saw one  
of them.

## From _Daily Ohio statesman._

#### 1867-10-28 &middot; [Columbus, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Columbus%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Two gentlemen from New York, one  
whom had been in Calitornia nearly a year,  
and the other just arrived, were acciden­  
tally overh eard in the following conver­  
sation at the Sutter House, Sacramento.  
The new comer was lamenting his condi­  
tion, and especially two beautiful daugh­  
ters who were just budding into woman­  
hood- when he asked the other if he had a  
family.  
Yes, sir, I have a wife and six children  
in New York--and I never saw one of  
them.&quot;  
After this the couple sat a few moments  
in silence, and then the interrogator again  
commenced:  
&quot;Were you ever blind, sir?&quot;  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;  
Another lapse of time.  
&quot;Did I understand you to say, sir, that  
you had a wife and six children living  
in New York, and had never seen one of  
them?&quot;  
&quot;Yes sir--I so stated it.&quot;  
Another and a long pause of silence.  
Then the interrogator again inquired:  
&quot;How can it be, sir, that you never saw  
one of them  
&quot;Why,&quot; was the response, &quot;one of them  
was born after I left.&quot;  
&quot;Oh, ah!&quot; and a general laugh follow  
ed.  
After that the first New Yorker was es  
pecially distinguished as the man who had  
six children and never saw one of them
</td></tr></table>

## From _The Ottawa free trader._

#### 1867-11-02 &middot; [Ottawa, Ill.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Ottawa%2C_Illinois)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
Begularly SolD.  
Two gentlemen from New York, one of whom  
has been in California nearly a year and the other  
just arrived, were accidentally overheard in the  
following conversation at the Sutter House, Sac­  
ramento. The new comer was lamenting his  
condition, and especially two beautiful daughters  
who were just budding into womanhood--when  
he asked the other if he had a family:  
&quot;Yes, sir, I have a wife and six children in  
New York--and I never saw one of them.&quot;  
After this the couple sat a few moments in  
silence, and then the interrogator again com­  
menced,  
&quot;Were you ever blind, sir?&quot;  
&quot;No, sir.&quot;  
Another lapes of time.  
&quot;Did I understand you to say, sir, that you  
had a wife and six children living in New York,  
and never saw one of them?&quot;  
&quot;Yes, sir--so I stated it.&quot;  
Another and a long pause of silence. Then the  
interrogator again inquired:  
&quot;How can it be, sir, that you never saw one of  
them?&quot;  
&quot;Why,&quot; was the response, &quot;one of them was  
born after I left.&quot;  
&quot;Oh! ah!&quot; and a general laugh followed.  
After that the first New Yorker was especially  
distinguished as the man who had six children  
and never saw one of them

 on the young man.  
Finding himself the object of so much attention, he  
himsel looked and discovered to his horror* some­  
thing white on which he sat, the end peeping out. He  
mistook its character. To cover it with his hand, and  
to tuck it nicely away, was the work of a few mo­  
ments. None in the car but the young lady knew  
that the “ something white” was a handkerchief, and  
hesitating still more to ask him, she lost it.

SYSTEMATICALLY SOLD.  
Two gentlemen from New York, one of whom had  
been in California nearly a year, and the other just  
arrived, were accidentally overheard in the following  
conversation at the Sutter house, Sacramento. The  
new Comer was lamenting his condition, when he  
asked the other if he had a family.  
“ Yes, sir, I have a wife and six children in New  
York, and never saw one of them.”  
After this, the couple sat a few moments in si­  
lence, and then the interrogator again commenced:  
“ Were you ever blind, sir.”,  
“No, sir.”  
Another lapse of time.  
“ Did I understand you to say, sir, that you had a  
wife and six children living in New York and had  
never seen one of them ?”  
“ Yes, sir—l so Stated it.”  
Another and a long pause of silence. Then the  
interrogator inquired :  
“How can it be, sir, that you never saw one of  
them ?”  
“ Why,” was the response, “ one of them was born  
after I left.”  
“Oil J ah!” and a general laugh followed.  
After that the first New Yorker was especially dis­  
tinguished as the man who had six children and  
never saw one of them

New York and had never seen one of them?”

Another and a long pause of silence.  
Then the interrogatoi inquired :   
“How can it be, sir, that you never saw  
one of them?”   
“Why,” was the response, “one of them  
was born after 1 left.”   
“Q, ah!” and a genera! laugh followed.   
After that the first New Yorker was es­  
pecially distinguished as tho man who had  
six children and never saw one of them.

Two gentlemen from New York&#x27;, one of  
whom has been in California nearly a year  
and the other just arrived, were acciden­

sation at the Sutter House, Sacramento.  
The new comer was lamenting his condi­

hood—when he asked the other if he had  
a family.  
“Yes, sir, I have a wife and six child­  
dren in New York—and I never saw one  
of them.”  
After this the couple sat a few mo­  
ments in silence, and then the interroga­  
tor again commenced :  
“Were you ever blind, sir ?”  
“No sir.”  
Another lapse of time.  
“Did I understand you to say, sir, that  
you had a wife and six children living in

To meet him on the ground.  
And still another and a fourth.  
Convene to scratch and bite.  
While fretted mortals toss and moan  
Throughout the dismal night.  
What boots It though a boot be thrown  
Ay, marry I or the jack?  
They scamper off. but in an hour  
Come smiling, yawping back.

Regularly Sold.?Two gentlemen from  
New York, one of whom had been in Cali.fornia  
nearly a year, and the other just arrived,  
were accidentally overheard in the following  
conversation at the Sutter House, Sacramento.  
The new comer was lamenting his  
condition, aud his folly in leaving an abundance  
at home, and especially two beautiful  
daughters who were just budding into womanhood?when  
he asked the other if he had  
a family.  
&quot;Yes, sir, I have. I have a wife and six  
children in New York?and I never saw one  
of them.&quot;

/ silence, and then the interrogator again com(  
menced:
